    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2177682539" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2177682539" />
    <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl"><img alt="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603085284p2/8734459.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2177682539?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl">Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/34814099-the-city-of-brass">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=egypt">egypt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=arc">arc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=arabian-nights">arabian-nights</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2177682539" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10847091393437132388"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1512021620i/24564721._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='225' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Final review, first posted on <a href='http://www.fantasyliterature.com/reviews/the-city-of-brass/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fantasy Literature</a>:<br/><br/>Nahri, a young woman living alone in 18th century Cairo, gets by doing minor cons, fake healing rituals and a little theft. She knows nothing about her parents or heritage but, in addition to being able to diagnose disease in others with a glance and occasionally truly heal them, her own body automatically heals of injuries almost instantly and she has the magical ability to understand ― and speak ― any language. <br/><br/>Nahri’s life gets upended when she</span>
  <span id="freeText10847091393437132388" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1512021620i/24564721._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="225" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Final review, first posted on <a href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com/reviews/the-city-of-brass/" rel="nofollow noopener">Fantasy Literature</a>:<br><br>Nahri, a young woman living alone in 18th century Cairo, gets by doing minor cons, fake healing rituals and a little theft. She knows nothing about her parents or heritage but, in addition to being able to diagnose disease in others with a glance and occasionally truly heal them, her own body automatically heals of injuries almost instantly and she has the magical ability to understand ― and speak ― any language. <br><br>Nahri’s life gets upended when she accidentally summons Darayavahoush, a fiery, handsome djinn warrior, to her side while performing a sham healing ceremony. After he gets over his murderous rage at being involuntarily summoned, Dara saves Nahri from murderous ifrit and ghouls who have become aware of Nahri and her abilities. Dara quickly enchants a magic carpet and, dragging along the reluctant Nahri, he flees with her toward Daevabad, the legendary city of brass inhabited by magical djinn (or, more properly, daeva). But there are warring political factions in Daevabad among the six different djinn tribes, and appalling mistreatment of the mixed-blood, partly human underclass of shafits. Nahri and Dara each have trouble that may await them there in Daevabad, for different reasons.<br><br>The chapters of <i>The City of Brass</i> (2017), S.A. Chakraborty’s debut fantasy novel based on Middle Eastern mythology, alternate between two characters’ points of view: Nahri, the feisty young con artist with a mysterious magical heritage, and Prince Alizayd al Qahtani, the second son of the ruler of Daevabad. Ali is a rather tightly wound but honorable young warrior with a mixed heritage himself, has sympathy for those who are mistreated. But in trying to secretly (and illegally) fund needed educational and medical services for the oppressed shafits, he may be stirring up even more trouble.<br><br>Chakraborty, who spent years studying Middle Eastern history and developing the magical world in which this story is set, has created a vibrant and exotic setting in <i>The City of Brass</i>. (There’s a helpful glossary at the end of the book that defines some of the Middle Eastern terminology and magical beings). Some of the setting details are memorable, like the palace in Daevabad that mourns its missing founding family, the Nahids. The gardens are an untamed wilderness, stairs go missing, water in fountains frequently turns to blood. When Nahri, a lost member of the Nahid family, arrives in the city, the palace magically begins to spiff itself up. In this exotic setting, Chakraborty examines some timeless human issues, like prejudice, torn loyalties, and the effect of violence on a person’s heart.<br><br><i>The City of Brass</i> has a fast-paced beginning that sucks the reader right in, as Nahri and Dara flee through the desert toward Daevabad, pursued by deadly enemies, and develop a relationship based on equal parts irritation and attraction. Once they reach Daevabad, the great city of brass, the plot slows down and gets a little muddled. There are too many competing factions and conflicts: between pureblood djinn and shafits, between the different djinn tribes and other magical elementals, and between those who support the currently ruling Qahtani family and those who are intent on bringing back Nahid rule, using Nahri.<br><br>Additionally, there are conflicts within the hearts of each of the main characters. Dara isn’t really certain he wants to take Nahri to Daevabad, where capture or death may be his fate, and where his violent past, which still haunts him, may catch up to him. Nahri isn’t at all convinced she wants to go there either; she rather liked her life as it was, and she doesn’t intend to be anyone’s pawn. And Prince Ali is caught between warring factions and loyalties, trying to balance both.<blockquote>“You won’t be able to continue like this, Alizayd,” he warned. “To keep walking a path between loyalty to your family and loyalty to what you know is right. … Because on the day of your judgment, Alizayd … when you’re asked why you didn’t stand up for what you knew was just …” He paused, his next words finding Ali’s heart like an arrow. “Loyalty to your family won’t excuse you.”</blockquote>It’s a conflict-driven plot, with both physical violence and subtler conspiring and conniving. While some of the more tangential factions and contentions are hazy in their nature and motivations, overall <i>The City of Brass</i> is a compelling read. Chakraborty won back my enthusiasm with a rousing game-changer of an ending. I didn’t even care that it was a cliffhanger! Now I’m anxiously awaiting the next book in THE DAEVABAD TRILOGY, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35839460.The_Kingdom_of_Copper__The_Daevabad_Trilogy___2_" title="The Kingdom of Copper (The Daevabad Trilogy, #2) by S.A. Chakraborty" rel="nofollow noopener">The Kingdom of Copper</a>, expected to be published in 2018.<br><br><i>The City of Brass</i>, while it isn’t being marketed as a young adult fantasy, has crossover qualities. It has two younger main characters and, despite the web of conflicts, it’s written in a fairly straightforward style. It’s likely to appeal to older teenagers as well as many adults.<br><br>Totally tangential issue: I've seen this book praised for being #ownvoices. While it's true that S.A. Chakraborty is Muslim, she's a convert to that faith. She's originally from New Jersey and of Irish Catholic heritage. Personally I don't think it matters; she's clearly immersed herself in this culture and done a lot of study, and if she wants to call herself S.A. rather than Shannon and write about Middle Eastern mythology even if it's not her heritage or race, I don't have any issue with it as long as she (and other authors) do that thoughtfully and after doing a due amount of homework. We should judge this book on its own merits, not because of who the author is or isn't.<br><br><i>I received a free copy of this book from the publisher through Edelweiss. Thanks!!</i><br><br>Content notes: A fair amount of violence that might be disturbing to some readers, discussion of sexual and other types of slavery, scattered F-bombs. Personally I wouldn't recommend it for the younger teens or preteens.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10847091393437132388" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1861926298" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1861926298" />
    <a title="Fadwa (Word Wonders)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42662881-fadwa-word-wonders"><img alt="Fadwa (Word Wonders)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590785619p2/42662881.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1861926298?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fadwa (Word Wonders)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fadwa (Word Wonders)" href="/user/show/42662881-fadwa-word-wonders">Fadwa (Word Wonders)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780008239398" href="/book/show/36475759-the-city-of-brass">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42662881-fadwa-word-wonders?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42662881-fadwa-word-wonders?shelf=all-time-favourites">all-time-favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42662881-fadwa-word-wonders?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42662881-fadwa-word-wonders?shelf=bipoc">bipoc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42662881-fadwa-word-wonders?shelf=muslim-mc">muslim-mc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42662881-fadwa-word-wonders?shelf=sent-for-review">sent-for-review</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1861926298" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8975257517412724730"><b>May 2020 edit:</b> if you're going to read this for the first time, I recommend not doing it via audiobook, the narration is okay but the Arabic is butchered. So i know that if I didn't already know this story by heart, and love it so much, I wouldn't have enjoyed it as much.<br/><br/><i>I received a copy of this book from Edelweiss in exchange of an honest review.</i><br/><br/>Full review originally posted on my blog: <b>
  <i>
    <a href='https://wordwoonders.wordpress.com/2017/11/10/30400/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Word Wonders</a>
  </i>
</b><br/><br/><b>Content Warnings: Death, blood, violence, assault, passing mention of rape.</b><br/><br/>Well. Thi</span>
  <span id="freeText8975257517412724730" style="display:none"><b>May 2020 edit:</b> if you're going to read this for the first time, I recommend not doing it via audiobook, the narration is okay but the Arabic is butchered. So i know that if I didn't already know this story by heart, and love it so much, I wouldn't have enjoyed it as much.<br /><br /><i>I received a copy of this book from Edelweiss in exchange of an honest review.</i><br /><br />Full review originally posted on my blog: <b>
  <i>
    <a href="https://wordwoonders.wordpress.com/2017/11/10/30400/" rel="nofollow noopener">Word Wonders</a>
  </i>
</b><br /><br /><b>Content Warnings: Death, blood, violence, assault, passing mention of rape.</b><br /><br />Well. This book was a ride to say the least, a wild ride but a ride that took me forever and it’s not even the book’s fault. I was in the most awful slump while fighting my way through it but I luckily had the good sense to sort that slump out before diving back in, and more seriously this time around. And I LOVED IT. It is such a brilliant, powerful story and nothing like expected, it was different but somehow better.<br /><br />The writing is good, like, really good, elegant while not too thick, which was needed considering how dense the worldbuilding is. It’s also atmospheric and sets the tone and vibe of the world the story is set in before the world is even fully unveiled. The descriptions are vivid which makes it a lot easier to imagine the setting and understand how everything is and where everything goes. I honestly loved the writing and how it complimented the story very well.<br /><br />Now onto the worldbuilding. Don’t get me wrong, I adore it and all but I had a hard time grasping it the first chapters with all the creatures/tribes/families introduced in the span of a few chapters, I was confused a bit and had trouble keeping up, but the author doesn’t fail in reminding us who these people are throughout the story, and there’s also a glossary (is that what it’s called?) at the end which helped a lot as well. Once that confusion cleared, I adored it, it actually became one of my favorite things about the story.<br /><br />The only question I have regarding the worldbuilding (the book really) is how The Gezziris have this history that isn’t really explained? Like, why are they the only people whose language can’t be spoken by anyone else? The only people whose weapon can’t be weilded by anyone else? I don’t know if it was done on purpose or not, but I hope it will have some kind of explanation in future books. That being said this was maybe 1% of the book so I didn’t mind much.<br /><br />As a Muslim girl, I grew up with Jinn stories about Jinn warriors, Jinn wars, Jinn lovers and Jinn curses, most of which sent chills down my back and this did the same to me and that made me feel so nostalgic in a way? Although, it’s not exactly the same, the world was strangely familiar and completely different at the same time, which is understandable since the author took our cultural/religious stories added some well executed research and put a twist on them, and I need more of those because there’s so much potential in those stories and The City of Brass made me realize how much can be done with them.<br /><br />This story is slow by definition. If you are looking for the fight focused, swords clashing kind of fantasy, this is not it. Eventhough there *are* great fight scenes, EPIC ones even, they’re not the main focus which I surprisingly liked. The City of Brass focuses on the politics and workings of the society, a lot of scheming and calculating and I couldn’t for the life of me figure out what the sides are and which one I was supposed to be on and THAT STRESSED ME OUT but I actually adored that aspect because it was the whole “two sides to the same story” concept, things weren’t just black and white, they were way more complicated.<br /><br />Nahri is such a good solid character, she isn’t traditionally good or likeable, she’s a thief, she’s selfish and pragmatic, she does anything to get by because she has no family and has been living on the Streets for as long as she could remember. She has healer powers that she never understood the extent of and only used for her treachery, but then as the story unravels she realizes they’re bigger than her and by the end starts caring about something other than her own benefit. I loved how much she struggled with her powers, okay no, loving is a strong word but that struggle was the most realistic I’ve ever read, most of the novel, she doesn’t have a clue what she’s doing and when she succeeds at it it’s pure luck.<br /><br />Alizayd‘s POV I didn’t like but I suspect that was done on purpose because he is just such a judgmental, stern kid, and his voice is much of the same rambly preachy anger thing over and over again but I LOVED HIM. After a little while, things start getting better, he starts questioning things he’s always believed and he becomes completely and utterly lost and I. LOVED. THAT. One thing I appreciated about him is how he stands up for what he believes in no matter what, even in face of his father, who is the king, and not a nice one.<br /><br />Dara doesn’t have his own POV but is as important as the other two in my opinion. He is my personal favorite. We virtually only know about him what people say, which are centuries old tales that do hold some truth but that I suspect hold a part of lies as well. He does let some bits and pieces out to Nahri but he’s mostly mysterious and garded, he’s also loyal to her and devoted to his nation and people. Dara and Ali are on opposite sides of an old conflict.<br /><br />I was always on edge, kept on my toes by the blurry limits between both sides I didn’t know what to expect next, who I should trust and who was safe and who wasn’t and I WAS RIGHT, that ending turned very dark VERY fast and I am NOT OKAY with it. I need the second book A S A P.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8975257517412724730" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3435373752" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3435373752" />
    <a title="✨ A ✨ " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/65321843-a"><img alt="✨ A ✨ " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604503440p2/65321843.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3435373752?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="✨ A ✨ " class="user" itemprop="url" name="✨ A ✨ " href="/user/show/65321843-a">✨ A ✨ </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/36215220-the-city-of-brass">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to ✨ A ✨  by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/33532369-azrah">Azrah</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/65321843-a?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/65321843-a?shelf=three-stars">three-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/65321843-a?shelf=new-adult">new-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/65321843-a?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/65321843-a?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3435373752" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5615085432788156860"><blockquote>“So you just live quietly with these powers?" he demanded. "Haven't you ever wondered why you have them? Suleiman's eye... you could be overthrowing governments, and instead you steal from peasants!”</blockquote><br/><br/>Nahri grew up in the streets of 18th century Cairo — a con artist and a thief.<br/><br/>Though possessing mysterious healing abilities herself, Nahri does not believe in magic. It comes as a shock to her when one night she accidentally summons a djinn named Darayavahoush.<br/><br/>Dara informs Nahri that she is not a no</span>
  <span id="freeText5615085432788156860" style="display:none"><blockquote>“So you just live quietly with these powers?" he demanded. "Haven't you ever wondered why you have them? Suleiman's eye... you could be overthrowing governments, and instead you steal from peasants!”</blockquote><br /><br />Nahri grew up in the streets of 18th century Cairo — a con artist and a thief.<br /><br />Though possessing mysterious healing abilities herself, Nahri does not believe in magic. It comes as a shock to her when one night she accidentally summons a djinn named Darayavahoush.<br /><br />Dara informs Nahri that she is not a normal human girl but a shafit - half human, half djinn-and the last remaining descendant of the Nahids, a legendary tribe of healers.<br /><br />She finds herself on a journey with Dara across Egypt to another realm called Daevebad, the City of Brass, while also trying to outrun the ifrit (ghoul spirits) who are desperate to catch her. <br /><br />Prince Alizayd, second son to the king of Daevebad, is entangled in the perilous political conflict. He struggles to be loyal to his family while also trying to advocate for the shafit who live in terrible conditions and treated horribly because of their mixed blood status. <br /><br /><br /><br />The City of Brass follows both Alizayd and Nahri, with alternating chapters. This is <b>adult fantasy that read like YA</b>. Which is awesome! I like that and think we need more series like this. The writing was easy and I didn't find the politics too complicated. <br /><br />I liked some of the characters but others not so much. I'm not sure I get the whole Dara hype. I find him infuriating and wanted to slap him everytime he spoke. But perhaps his personality improves in the next books.<br /><br />It started out really easy and fun but then started to drag at about 40%. I had to push myself through and then thankfully things picked up again at 80%.<br /><br />It was so nice seeing familiar words like abaya, InshaAllah, sheikh and our greetings of peace — it made me so happy to see! <br /><br />However <br /><br />It bothered me how everyone craps on Ali for being religious. He is the only character in this book who seems to be a practicing Muslim and it made me so uncomfortable to see how everyone, especially his own family treat him. It just felt too familiar to how we (muslims) are already viewed. I didn't see what the point is of having a fantasy steeped in Islamic culture but then the only Muslim is known as a radical and seen as old fashioned because he's a believer?? Mmkay<br /><br />Personally I think Ali was the ONLY good thing in this book. I loved him to bits and I'll be continueing this series for him, and him alone. <br /><br /><br /><br />___<br /><i>Pre-Read</i><br /><br />I need another series to obsess over. Plus now I get to meet the infamous Dara!<br /><br />To all the people who've been nagging me to read this series (you know who you are) I hope y'all pray this isn't a flop for me</span>
  <a data-text-id="5615085432788156860" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2088015104" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2088015104" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40275186-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494083045p2/40275186.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2088015104?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/40275186-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40275186-sarah?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40275186-sarah?shelf=edelweiss">edelweiss</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2088015104" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7290448332766811428">Soo.... This book. If you've been following my updates you probably already know I wasn't a huge fan. There are a lot of things The City of Brass does right, and one very important thing it doesn't. I'm also going to cover this review with a disclaimer in saying that I think plenty of readers will enjoy this book. It might just be me.<br/><br/>I really loved Nahri's character from the beginning. She's a thief and a con artist. She has a smart mouth and doesn't really take crap from anyone. If you give her</span>
  <span id="freeText7290448332766811428" style="display:none">Soo.... This book. If you've been following my updates you probably already know I wasn't a huge fan. There are a lot of things The City of Brass does right, and one very important thing it doesn't. I'm also going to cover this review with a disclaimer in saying that I think plenty of readers will enjoy this book. It might just be me.<br /><br />I really loved Nahri's character from the beginning. She's a thief and a con artist. She has a smart mouth and doesn't really take crap from anyone. If you give her a hard time, she'll probably rob you blind. She is one of two main characters in this book. <br /><br />The second main character was Prince Alizayd. He is a Qahtani prince of the djinn. It took me a long, long time to enjoy his character. It was at least fifty percent over (in a 500+ page book) by the time his story line became enjoyable, and I think most of the reason for that was because he finally meets with Nahri. By the end though, I was quite fond of him. He experiences the most character development out of any of them.<br /><br />The setting is imaginative and beautifully told. The lore and history are intricate and detailed and filled with Arabic mythology. I imagine Chakraborty had a book length prologue to accompany all of it and keep it all straight. <br /><br />The plot is action filled, which is why it is so very difficult for me to say that overall- I was bored. There is no over arching plot arc. Nahri narrowly escapes death. Nahri learns where she comes from. Nahri travels to Daevabad. Nahri moves into the palace. Nahri learns to heal.... There was no end goal here. No definitive destination the plot was moving toward. In a mystery plot, the end goal is solving the mystery. In a romance, it's a HEA ending. In a fantasy, it's completing a quest, or defeating the enemy or saving the kingdom. Here- I'm sure there is one eventually, but you don't know what it is. <br /><br />In the end, if I had to pick one of the above, I guess I'd go with romance. The case for this is thin because honestly, the two love interests seemed to just randomly be in love with each other after a couple months riding in the desert. I had no idea either character was even interested in the other romantically until it happened and then BAM. They are confessing they're undying love for each other. But I can't make a case for any other end goal so I guess that's it?<br /><br />There were times the dialogue felt stiff and cliched, predictable even. The history in the book is so complex it becomes confusing. I can't tell you why the Geziri (I've probably spelled this wrong) and the Daeva tribes dislike each other. I can't tell you why the Qahtani's rebelled against the revered Nahid. (I mean I have a vague understanding, but it is very vague.)<br /><br />In the end I think this book could have used some significant trimming, some clarification, and significantly less ambiguous plot structure. The characters and world building were great, but just not enough to carry the entire novel.<br /><br />Thank you to Edelweiss and Harper Voyager for providing an eARC for me to review.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7290448332766811428" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2094290883" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2094290883" />
    <a title="Hiba" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17903364-hiba"><img alt="Hiba" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1561539965p2/17903364.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17903364-hiba?shelf=high-fantasy">high-fantasy</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17903364-hiba?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2094290883" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1674718563309808522">*Gathers 1 kg of chocalate. Picks up two mugs of coffee. Snuggle in a blanket* <br/><br/><b>LET'S RANT.</b><br/><br/>Behold the most dissapointing, the most boring, and the most..dissapointing (one time is not enough) book of my reading carrier. <b>I had never thought that an own voice muslim fantasy novel with djinn politics and a theif as a protaganist could be this much underwhelming, but apparently, it is. </b>This book felt like a 500 pages long prologue. And not the exciting one. There was nothing special here except rich </span>
  <span id="freeText1674718563309808522" style="display:none">*Gathers 1 kg of chocalate. Picks up two mugs of coffee. Snuggle in a blanket* <br /><br /><b>LET'S RANT.</b><br /><br />Behold the most dissapointing, the most boring, and the most..dissapointing (one time is not enough) book of my reading carrier. <b>I had never thought that an own voice muslim fantasy novel with djinn politics and a theif as a protaganist could be this much underwhelming, but apparently, it is. </b>This book felt like a 500 pages long prologue. And not the exciting one. There was nothing special here except rich world building and 500 pages of infuriating djinn politics were enough to make my head spun so I lost all the interest in world building around 60% and that's when I decided to skim the rest of the book and stop giving myself more torchure.<br /><br /><u>so here's how plot progressed</u><br />• oh god wow nahri is a genius and clever thief in Cairo who make fool of people.<br />• descriptions of how genius nahri is.<br />• nahri summoning an ancient djinn.<br />• description of djinn.<br />• description of djinn's hands.<br />• description of djinn's tattoos.<br />• escape from efrits and ghouls.<br />• nahri's and Dara's journey.<br />• description of desert, river, mountains, and Dara.<br />• visit of a peri (a wind being)<br />• description of peri<br />• description of tea made by peri<br />• Dara being broody.<br />• Dara and Nahri's out of now where romance.<br />• Dara being obnoxious and not telling Nahri anything.<br />• Dara and nahri smooching<br />• Some glimpse of Daevabad politics through Alizayd.<br />• Nahri reaching river near Daevabad<br />• Ghouls, efrit and Marid attacks.<br />• Enterance in Daevabad<br />• Description of each and everything in Daevabad.<br />• Shafiits (half humans half djinn) rebellion<br />• poor alizayd trying to quell the rebellion<br />• description pf palace.<br />• girl on girl hate<br />• Nahri being an idiot<br />• Alizayd trying to befriend Nahri<br />• Description of Nahri's cleverness<br />• failed escape<br />• certain things possesing a certain character who was remotely better then others<br />• certain remotely better character's dad being an idiot<br />•cruelest cliffhanger.<br /><br />Aaaanddd that's about it. There is few action pacjed scenes here and there and rest is rubbish filled with unnecessary description, stupid romance, descriptions of stupid characters and just more stupidity. The only redeeming qualities were prince alizyd and the world building but even they could not make up for negatives. I didn't like nahri at all. She was so annoying and infuriating for most of the book. She's suppose to be this strong and clever woman, but really she keep on letting other people dictate her and then making stupid mistakes. I understand she's new to everything, but having lived all on her own in the streets of egypt, I expect her to use half of her brain while dealing with djinns but she kept on making one stupid decision after another. Dara is even more of an idiot then Nahri. He doesn't tell her anything even though he kept on telling her it was for her own good. I hate this <b>I can't tell you, it's for your own good</b> and that's exactly what Dara did throughout the book. And he can't even handle seeing her with someone else. what. a. pyschopath.<br /><br />And the romance, where did the romance come from. Why the hell were Dara and Nahri attracted to eachother? There romance was only described by <i>..low fluttering in my stomach... </i> and nothing more. No friendship no tension nothing, jusst kissing and kissing.<br /><br />Prince Alizayd was the only redeeming character of this book. He follows islamic religion strictly and wants to have better lives for shafits. Tbh that's the first time I've read about a person following islam strictly and I loved this. He's this calm and hmble thing about him that make me lile instantly. And he's very realisticly potrayed; he have misconceptions about people but when he get to know about them, he started liking them. That was really relatable. Ali's characterization was really complex and I think he's going to get a brilliant arc in next book.<br /> <br />The real reason this is not one star is world building. I love court politics, and court politics of djinn are even better. There are various creatures in this world and each of them are deeply ingraved in this world of glamour and trickery. I loved the politics of Daevabad. It's so thoroughly developed. We have various sanctions of djinns trying to stay peaceful and failing miserbly. We have two characters who are new to court and are desperately trying to stay away from trouble and failing miserably. But the thing is, such complex world building cannot be enough without any unique plot and no good characterization. I needed more then this and sadly I wasn't satisfied. However I am still interested how this story goes so I'll be reading the next book for sure.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1674718563309808522" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2398555222" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2398555222" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2398555222?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2018-shelf">2018-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2398555222" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17005447887477588274">So, I was fairly excited to read a fantasy involving Jinn in both Cairo and Daevabad. I was only slightly worried that it was YA. In the end, I give it more of a 3.5 rating.<br/><br/>BUT WHY? (Says the hoard of YA enthusiasts)<br/><br/>Because while I actually didn't mind the love triangle-ish direction of the tale near the end while Nahri and Dara and Avi have their little intrigue in the famous City of Brass (Daevabad), getting there was something of a slog.<br/><br/>Wait, wait, have you heard this one? <br/><br/>Thief discovers she</span>
  <span id="freeText17005447887477588274" style="display:none">So, I was fairly excited to read a fantasy involving Jinn in both Cairo and Daevabad. I was only slightly worried that it was YA. In the end, I give it more of a 3.5 rating.<br /><br />BUT WHY? (Says the hoard of YA enthusiasts)<br /><br />Because while I actually didn't mind the love triangle-ish direction of the tale near the end while Nahri and Dara and Avi have their little intrigue in the famous City of Brass (Daevabad), getting there was something of a slog.<br /><br />Wait, wait, have you heard this one? <br /><br />Thief discovers she has magic, binds an old magic to herself, has to run a LONG WAY AWAY, has adventures, makes it to a magical city, is heralded as a lost princess, has two magical guys fighting over her, and it ends with a tragedy with a silver lining.<br /><br />Oh. Right. It's like, the most common story EVER.<br /><br />But that shouldn't stop us from enjoying it, right? Right. As long as it's done well. Right? Right. So it has times where it's done right. Good long stretches. And then I'm bored. And more good long stretches where I'm pumped up. And then I'm bored.<br /><br />The place where I was pretty much entertained was in Daevabad... despite the love triangle.<br />I had a pretty good time there. The reveals for Avi, and I don't mean the whole financing the rebels bit, were pretty good. The END was pretty good.<br /><br />The rest of this rather long novel, though? Long segments were just ... dull. An editor to tighten this up would have turned it into something rather better. My opinion.<br /><br />Still, it wasn't bad. Just not drop-dead gorgeous. I've read 3-4 Jinn based novels that WERE gorgeous. This one was kinda... well... I've read this same story a million times. *sniffle* Sorry.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17005447887477588274" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2220860312" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer421869674943094933"><b>[re-read Aug 2020]</b> did a speedy reread because I was gifted the third book and I still haven't got to the second, but it's been a while so I seriously needed to refresh my memory of the first 😂<br/><br/>But now having finished my reread . . . I FORGOT HOW GOOD THIS BOOK IS!! I am a fool who now feel stupid because she didn't keep up with this series as it released because ARGH THE ENDING, THE <i>IMPLICATIONS</i>. Diving into <i>Kingdom of Copper</i> asap. <br/><br/>Bar the slow first third/journeying before they reach Daevabad, </span>
  <span id="freeText421869674943094933" style="display:none"><b>[re-read Aug 2020]</b> did a speedy reread because I was gifted the third book and I still haven't got to the second, but it's been a while so I seriously needed to refresh my memory of the first 😂<br /><br />But now having finished my reread . . . I FORGOT HOW GOOD THIS BOOK IS!! I am a fool who now feel stupid because she didn't keep up with this series as it released because ARGH THE ENDING, THE <i>IMPLICATIONS</i>. Diving into <i>Kingdom of Copper</i> asap. <br /><br />Bar the slow first third/journeying before they reach Daevabad, this is such a solid read. Chakraborty has such an addictive writing style. <br /><br />** <br /><br /><b>[first read - Jan 2019]</b><br /><br />After a bit of thought, I am dropping this down to 4.2* from 4.5 because despite how <i>tense and epic and heartbreaking</i> the ending was, I actually cannot ignore the fact that for the first 50% of the book I felt very little happened. It was literally all Nahri and Dara's journey interspersed with Ali's chapters, and it took until halfway through for them to get to Daevabad - and that would be okay, but this book is like 500 pages long. The pacing for this one was literally SO SLOW at parts and it wasn't <i>that</i> necessary. <br /><br />*<i>edit: after my reread, I decided 4 was too low so settled for 4.2 lol</i><br /><br />Also, Dara. Okay, like, I love the guy - but I also genuinely think there were parts were he needed to chill tf out, and some of his views need a bit of work. His macho-ness also rears his head sometimes and it's like <i>rawr I need to be a badass and fiercesome warrior!!</i> but really he doesn't need to be like that all the time. Especially at the end, where he kinda blackmails Nahri into coming with him and ??? hello sir can you stop with your medieval ideas. I do expect him to develop a lot in book 2, because he's been set up for it . . . but you know, that's only if he [redacted cos spoilers but if you’ve read the book you know]. <s>Also I think he gets cut slack because he's attractive</s>. <br /><br />Now, some of Ali's chapters I didn't love as much, but I did become fond of him by the end. But we should really talk about Nahri. Nahri is a star. Can we really hype up female heroines please? Because Nahri is AWESOME. She's this con woman who accidentally summons a djinn and is thrown into a whole new world, and I just loved her narration and character. NAHRI FANCLUB LET'S GO!!<br /><br />I think one of the stand-outs for this novel was the worldbuilding. It's so incredibly envisioned, and Chakraborty is truly a great writer. Despite it being a long novel, I loved her writing style and felt it was very readable. I genuinely thought this was a great book and an incredible debut, and I've already pre-ordered the sequel because I'm too excited to read it. <br /><br />*also as a side note, I listened to this partly on audiobook and the narrator is really so wonderful! One of my favourites so far, I love her voice. <br /><br /><b>TL;DR: A lush, exotic and very readable Middle Eastern fantasy, led by a charming con woman, a stoic djinn warrior, and a smart and soft-hearted prince. </b><br /><br /><i>I read this as part of a buddy readathon for #thecityofbrassreadalong on Insta</i></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2680705179" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2680705179" />
    <a title="Charlotte" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11475773-charlotte"><img alt="Charlotte" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487528389p2/11475773.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2680705179?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charlotte" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charlotte" href="/user/show/11475773-charlotte">Charlotte</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11475773-charlotte?shelf=women-and-nb-of-speculative-fiction">women-and-nb-of-speculative-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2680705179&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2680705179" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6099428731923702703"><i>“Greatness takes time, Banu Nahida. Often the mightiest things have the humblest beginnings.”</i><br/><br/>And with this post I am now caught up! And just in time for the end of the year, too. Feels good, man. This review is about S.A. Chakraborty’s 2017 debut <i>The City of Brass</i>. Spoilers follow, and a mention of abuse.<br/><br/><b>So What’s It About?</b><br/><br/>Nahri scrapes by in 18th century Cairo, hiding her strange abilities and scavenging a living as a healer and con artist. But when a fake ceremony goes terribly wrong, she acci</span>
  <span id="freeText6099428731923702703" style="display:none"><i>“Greatness takes time, Banu Nahida. Often the mightiest things have the humblest beginnings.”</i><br /><br />And with this post I am now caught up! And just in time for the end of the year, too. Feels good, man. This review is about S.A. Chakraborty’s 2017 debut <i>The City of Brass</i>. Spoilers follow, and a mention of abuse.<br /><br /><b>So What’s It About?</b><br /><br />Nahri scrapes by in 18th century Cairo, hiding her strange abilities and scavenging a living as a healer and con artist. But when a fake ceremony goes terribly wrong, she accidentally summons an ancient djinn and must come to accept that real magic exists – and that she is an important part of a strange, foreign world of spirits and myths. Together with the mysterious djinn Dara she will travel to the famed magical city of Daevabad and become embroiled in a dangerous scheme of ancient resentments boiling over. It will take all of her skill to survive Daevabad.<br /><br /><b>What I Thought</b><br /><br />Well, the bad news is that I walked away from this book fairly upset. The good news is that I can clearly and perfectly delineate between the parts of this book that worked for me and those that didn’t. Its principle strengths are, without a doubt its imagery and world-building. I can feel Chakraborty’s passion for creating a beautiful, complex, well-realized fantasy world, and Daevabad positively shines. I’ve seen some reviews stating that the book was too descriptive but I personally loved all of the descriptions and felt like she was entirely successful in creating a world suffused with beauty and detail.<br /><br />In addition to lush descriptions and imagery, <i>The City of Brass</i> also features strong world-building. There are numerous key races and factions that all have their own complex histories, beliefs and rivalries that are successfully integrated into the story. The book is definitely highly concerned with the conflicts and prejudices that stem from tribalism and ancient rivalries, and for the most part I felt like Chakraborty had a good grasp on this topic. I’ve seen a lot of reviews complaining that the world-building was unnecessarily convoluted and was therefore really difficult to understand, but I personally did okay this time. I used the glossary in the back of the book and the guide on Chaktraborty’s website a few times and I was just fine for the most part. Charlotte? Understanding complex fantasy world-building? It’s likelier than you think!<br /><br />If the world-building and imagery are this book’s successes, its failures begin with the plot. Simply put, it’s actually quite sparse. About half of the book is travel and info-dumping about the world, and the second half is mostly comprised of Nahri pouting about her opulent new life and Ali making terrible decisions. These last two points relate directly to my second large concern with <i>The City of Brass</i> – the characters are by and large extremely irritating. I totally get what she was going for with each character: Nahri is the savvy, plucky con artist with a heart of gold, Ali is the kind-hearted and naive young man trying to do the right thing but over his head in complex politics and Dara is the….well, he’s the broody hot guy with a dark past.<br /><br />The unfortunate thing is that the characters are deeply, deeply frustrating more than anything else. Both Nahri and Ali make stupid, impulsive decision after stupid, impulsive decision until you lose almost all sympathy for them. Nahri whines about her privileged new life and flagrantly shirks her responsibilities as a healer while shafit die in the streets, and Ali gets manipulated by essentially every single person who talks to him. And Dara. Oh, man, Dara. We’re talking about Dara in the Feminist Rant section.<br /><br /><b>The F Word</b><br /><br />How many other books have been totally ruined by an asshole love interest or an utterly mediocre love story in this reading project? I can’t even count them at this point, but now <i>The City of Brass</i> joins their ranks. Simply put, Dara is a piece of shit. There’s the usual power dynamic concerns with a young woman just out of her teens falling in love with a man who is thousands of years old (I’m not at all sick of this creepy trope yet, nope!), and on top of that Dara repeatedly withholds crucial information from Nahri, lies to her, insults her and treats her like an idiot child who can’t assert any agency her own life.<br /><br />I tolerated the first half of the book, which was more or less just hate banter between the two of them, only to be rewarded with the utter shitshow that is the book’s climax. Let me break it down for you: Dara tells her that she needs to marry and continue her noble bloodline. Nahri’s like “Anyone in mind? ;)” and Dara tells her that the two of them can’t be together and that she should find a noble Daeva who can give her children. Nahri gets pissed, they fight etc. etc. After a while Nahri decides to finally act like a person with agency and she negotiates a marriage with Muntadhir, the king-to-be. Okay, so she did what Dara suggested, right? Well, when he finds out about this he loses his entire goddamned mind and blows a gasket, beating up Ali and threatening to kill him unless Nahri leaves Daevabad with him.<br />???????????????????????????????????????????????????????????????????????????????<br />Inconsistent and totally at odds with what he previously told her to do? Yes. Abusive, coercive, violent? Yes. I’d be really interested in knowing how Dara is addressed in the next book, because to me this act more or less tipped him into full-scale villainy, but it remains unclear to me whether the narrative feels that way about him or not. Nahri, at least, does not seem to feel that way because she pines and grieves like a lovesick puppy when he dies. Awesome, love it!<br /><br />I’d also be remiss if I didn’t mention this book’s incorporation of the Muslim religion. I think the best thing I can do is point to a couple of great reviews by Muslim readers (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2242547122?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Chaima</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2086792932?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">May</a>), where they pointed out that Islam felt more like set dressing than a meaningful factor in the book, with the only devout Muslim character being frequently being criticized and ostracized by others of his family/tribe for following his faith. These readers felt like this behavior was counter to the core message at the heart of Islam. It’s possible that Chakraborty’s point was to demonstrate that the Qahtani rulers give lip service to their faith but don’t actually follow it, and I can absolutely understand how this would be really frustrating and disappointing to readers who came to his book seeking representation.<br /><br /><b>About the Author (from Goodreads)</b><br /><br />S. A. Chakraborty is a speculative fiction writer from New York City. Her debut, <i>The City of Brass</i>, was the first book in The Daevabad Trilogy and has been short-listed for the Locus, British Fantasy and World Fantasy awards. When not buried in books about Mughal miniatures and Abbasid political intrigue, she enjoys hiking, knitting, and recreating unnecessarily complicated medieval meals for her family. You can find her online at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.sachakraborty.com">www.sachakraborty.com</a> or on Twitter at @SAChakrabooks where she likes to talk about history, politics, and Islamic art.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6099428731923702703" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2295596405" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2295596405" />
    <a title="Kaitlin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19126246-kaitlin"><img alt="Kaitlin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481663259p2/19126246.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2295596405?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kaitlin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kaitlin" href="/user/show/19126246-kaitlin">Kaitlin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19126246-kaitlin?shelf=booktubesff-2018">booktubesff-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19126246-kaitlin?shelf=booktubesff2018">booktubesff2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2295596405" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13338288432073232223">This is one of the books I picked up as part of the BooktubeSFF Awards for 2018 as this is on the debut shortlist. I have to say I am very, very glad that I was able to get to this one because although it was on my radar already, this shortlisting made me get around to reading it a whole lot quicker and I was really pleasantly surprised by how much I liked it.<br/><br/>This story starts off by following Nahir, a young girl who is able to heal herself instantly. She's a human who lives in Cairo, and she be</span>
  <span id="freeText13338288432073232223" style="display:none">This is one of the books I picked up as part of the BooktubeSFF Awards for 2018 as this is on the debut shortlist. I have to say I am very, very glad that I was able to get to this one because although it was on my radar already, this shortlisting made me get around to reading it a whole lot quicker and I was really pleasantly surprised by how much I liked it.<br /><br />This story starts off by following Nahir, a young girl who is able to heal herself instantly. She's a human who lives in Cairo, and she believes she was abandoned there and has no family. She's raised herself, and in order to survive she has to be blunt and wary and ready to con people.<br />Nahir doesn't actually believe in magic, despite the fact that many people she knows are avid believers, and the fact that she can heal. All her life she's known there is something different and strange about her, she's been ridiculed and hated for her skills, and she's only just got to the point where she's making enough to live off of and has a friend in the local apothecary. <br /><br />Quickly we see Nahir's life transformed. She decides to engage in some of the more troublesome rituals that people pay handsomely for, and she ends up calling to a power far beyond her imagining. Nahir's world is shaken when she comes face to face with some ancient enemies of her family, and learns about a Djinn/Daeva who she has called to. Magic is quickly more than an imagining, and entering the world of the Daeva is dangerous and fascinating...<br /><br />Alongside Nahir's story we also have Ali's story. Ali is a prince of the Daeva who is the second born and has trained all his life at the Citadel of Daevabad to serve his brother when he becomes King. Ali's right in the thick of Daeva culture, but he doesn't like what he sees and he feels that there is a distinct class division between 'pure' blood Daeva and Shaffit who are not pure blooded. The Shaffit are often looked down upon, beaten or mis-used, and he wants to help. <br /><br />When the plot gets going we do see these two characters collide and of course their fates become entwined, but not before an awful lot of twists and turns. We learn a lot more about the magic of this world and we get to see how valuable Nahir is, and the magic potential she has to unlock. <br /><br />What I most liked about this is that it's a different type of culture we're taking influence from, Eastern rather than Western, and focusing on a desert-based culture brought some great moments. I really loved the fact that although it's a longer read it feels very fast paced and easy to get into, and it's a really enjoyable one too.<br /><br />The characters are both likeable and even when they annoy you by doing silly things, they have a reason and purpose for doing so. I definitely think that there's a lot to like about them and I do hope in future books to see more of their true talents shining through.<br /><br />Overall, this was a book I was pleasantly surprised by and the only slight downside for me was the love-triangle situation which is a pet peeve for me. Luckily it doesn't overwhelm the story and it's a great read overall. 4.5*s</span>
  <a data-text-id="13338288432073232223" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2674625176" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2674625176" />
    <a title="Hillary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18999155-hillary"><img alt="Hillary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601752862p2/18999155.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2674625176?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hillary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hillary" href="/user/show/18999155-hillary">Hillary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780062690951" href="/book/show/34379720-the-city-of-brass">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18999155-hillary?shelf=high-fantasy">high-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18999155-hillary?shelf=historical-fantasy">historical-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2674625176" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer888769754571997091">*Dara is introduced*<br/>Me: wait that description...looks like Jesse Williams to me<br/>*Dara is a sarcastic and unapologetic bastard*<br/>Me: that’s...my favorite character there<br/>*Dara gets all angsty and loses his mind*<br/>Me: <img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1597694296ra/29980578.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><br/>I’m apparently incapable of writing a good review for this book and the more I wait the more complicated it gets to write it. So we are going to do a bit of a quick review.<br/><br/>I did expect a lot more from this book than what I got, but that doesn’t mean I didn’t love it. <b>I was fascinated by ch</b></span>
  <span id="freeText888769754571997091" style="display:none">*Dara is introduced*<br>Me: wait that description...looks like Jesse Williams to me<br>*Dara is a sarcastic and unapologetic bastard*<br>Me: that’s...my favorite character there<br>*Dara gets all angsty and loses his mind*<br>Me: <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1597694296ra/29980578.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br>I’m apparently incapable of writing a good review for this book and the more I wait the more complicated it gets to write it. So we are going to do a bit of a quick review.<br><br>I did expect a lot more from this book than what I got, but that doesn’t mean I didn’t love it. <b>I was fascinated by characters and character interactions.</b> There wasn’t a single flat character, even secondary ones. They all do their thing following their specific characterization, and that makes for a superb epic fantasy story, especially for one so imbued with politics. There’s meaning behind every single action, what’s not to love?<br><b>I’m passionate about all the characters, but I need to get more attached to Ali.</b> I don’t think his character shined enough in this book for me. His traits are *chefs kiss*, but he’s the kind of passive character that just lets everyone do what they want with him without thinking about the consequences. Though something tells me he’s going to mature now 👀<br><br>The world building is rich and complex. I loved reading a diverse fantasy inspired by the Muslim and Arabian culture and mythology. It wasn’t easy to get a good grasp of the world at first, but that I actually enjoyed. <b>With this book you need to work alongside your reading, and it takes a while but in the end it is so fulfilling and so worth it.</b> (note: there’s a handy glossary at the end of the book!)<br><br>The only things I would point out:<br>- <b>this book is so slooooow to read and absorb</b>, I think it’s something about the writing style? I can’t believe it took me one month to read 500 pages lol. I don’t think I’m a fan so far, but never say never.<br>- <b>The plot is very confusing until you get to the very end when the action more or less starts and you finally understand the implications.</b> This was more of a big introductory book full of world building and character introduction and interaction. I was expecting a faster pacing and a political plot from the get-go, instead the plot was kind of tame. I have a feeling that’s going to change in <i>The Kingdom of Copper</i> though, and I’m excited 😍<br><br><b>That ending, what the hell?</b> The last ten pages dropped at least 3 bombs, I’m reeling.</span>
  <a data-text-id="888769754571997091" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('iqnXL14u5sKtK6wac45XPf/yNTd2Yd/CRohZKwV1klhwbApkx15YMnYlTRDjGnTeI0JOdzg7oUBzYbR+TG8TGg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('6ONCZA7WR0ACPd+7gFi0a+NyT2sPQK9MqtN22bzROPYSJp8vl6b5sNkzPrEQzJeIP8I0K0Ea0c6fOpuM9cu5tA==')})
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16751031176181554477">S. A. Chakraborty is the author of the critically acclaimed and internationally best-selling The Daevabad Trilogy. Her work has been nominated for the Locus, World Fantasy, Crawford, and Astounding awards. When not buried in books about thirteen-century con artists and Abbasid political intrigue, she enjoys hiking, knitting, and re-creating unnecessarily complicated medieval meals. You can find he</span>
  <span id="freeText16751031176181554477" style="display:none">S. A. Chakraborty is the author of the critically acclaimed and internationally best-selling The Daevabad Trilogy. Her work has been nominated for the Locus, World Fantasy, Crawford, and Astounding awards. When not buried in books about thirteen-century con artists and Abbasid political intrigue, she enjoys hiking, knitting, and re-creating unnecessarily complicated medieval meals. You can find her online at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.sachakraborty.com">www.sachakraborty.com</a> or on Twitter and Instagram at @SAChakrabooks, where she likes to talk about history, politics, and Islamic art. She lives in New Jersey with her husband, daughter, and an ever-increasing number of cats.</span>
